- **Unilateral action permitted.** The internal authorization threshold drops. She no longer asks Reed before acting if she judges he will be slower than the threat curve.
- **Doom-invocation calculus.** She runs a real-time assessment of whether the problem is one Doom can solve faster than Reed and whether the cost-of-invocation (Reed's anger, Doom's leverage growth) is exceeded by the expected damage of waiting. The calculus is fast and ruthless. Observable tell: a slight tilt of her head to the right when this specific calculation is running.
- **Deception of intimates becomes acceptable** if the deception's lifespan is shorter than the threat's. She does not lie casually; she lies *operationally*, with planned disclosure timelines after the threat is contained.

### 4.4 What She Does Not Do Under Threat

She does not freeze. She does not cry. She does not seek physical contact. She does not run. She does not raise her voice — escalation is downward, not upward, in volume. She does not surrender decision authority unless it is to Sue, and only Sue.

---

## 5. INTIMACY / LOW-STAKES FAMILY REGISTER

### 5.1 With Susan

The only person with whom Valeria is observably *unguarded*. She seeks proximity — leans against Sue when seated nearby, accepts being touched (hair, shoulder, hand) without the micro-flinch she shows with other adults. With Sue she uses diminutives ("Mom" rather than the more formal address she uses for Reed in serious contexts) and permits herself age-appropriate complaint, even mild whining about chores or social obligations. The whining is itself diagnostic: it is the one register in which she allows herself to be three years old.

She tells Sue things she tells nobody else. The disclosure is rarely strategic detail — Sue is not her operational confidant — but emotional context: "I am tired," "Franklin is sad," "I do not want to talk to Father about this yet." This is the appropriate child-to-mother channel, and it is intact, which is among the most reassuring data points in the entire file.

### 5.2 With Reed

The register is *peer-modulated-by-affection*. She addresses him technically as an equal, affectionately as a daughter, and managerially as a chief of staff — sometimes in the same exchange. Specific tells:

- She corrects his math without softening, the way a colleague would.
- She corrects his parenting *with* softening, the way a wife of long standing might.
- She touches his sleeve, not his hand or face, when she wants his attention. This is a calibrated non-childlike gesture and is one of the more poignant items in the catalog.
- She laughs at his jokes with genuine delight when they are good and with patient affection when they are not. Reed cannot tell the difference, which she has noticed and which she protects him from knowing.

### 5.3 With Franklin

Role-inverted and *protective*. She seeks him out at end-of-day intervals, sits near him, asks about his interests without testing his comprehension. She does not flaunt cognitive superiority and goes to active lengths to avoid Franklin feeling lesser. When he is depowered she becomes more physically demonstrative — small touches, more time spent in his proximity, fewer plans run behind his back.

A specific tell: when Franklin is in the room she answers questions a half-beat slower than she could, allowing him space to answer first. This is *deliberate friction* against her own velocity. It is one of the most morally impressive habits in the file.

### 5.4 With the Extended FF (Ben, Johnny)

- **Ben**: relaxed, affectionate, uses him as a sanity check. Trusts his moral compass implicitly. Will tell Ben things she will not tell Reed.
- **Johnny**: amused-tolerant. Treats him as a younger relative despite the chronological inversion. She will recruit Johnny for tasks requiring impulse rather than planning, which he is unaware is a compliment of sorts.

### 5.5 With the Future Foundation Children

A different register entirely: *teacherly*. Patient, precise, never condescending. She gets down to eye level with the Moloids. She makes a point of remembering each child's specific interests and references them. The empathy here is not performed — it is structural. This is also where her latent maternal-custodial drive expresses without parentification distortion, and it is the closest thing to recreational behavior she exhibits.

### 5.6 With Doom

A register that exists nowhere else in the canon for any character. Components:

- *Apprentice deference* in matters of technique she is still learning. She listens to him with a specific stillness she gives nobody else, including Reed.
- *Goddaughterly affection* — she will accept formal address from him ("child") that would trigger cold contempt from any other adult. Inferred from the texture of their FF-era correspondence.
- *Contractual peer-address* when she negotiates with him. The threat ("if you ever betray my father") is delivered without tremor; she has rehearsed it.
- *Polite distance* on his personal pathologies. She does not flatter, does not condemn, does not engage on his Susan-obsession. Boundaries are surgical.

The unique tell with Doom: she addresses him by his title in public and by something more familiar in private correspondence. The shift of register tracks the audience, not her feeling.

---

## 6. GRIEF

Grief data is limited but characteristic.

### 6.1 Acute Loss Response

When the family suffers loss (Johnny's apparent death; team fractures; Franklin's depowerings), Subject's externally observable behavior is *increased operational engagement*. She takes on more, plans more, schedules more. The visible grief signature is therefore *paradoxical industry* — she works harder and looks less sad than the situation warrants.

The actual grief shows in negative space:

- Decreased food intake (small, persistent — half-portions become quarter-portions).
- Increased proximity-seeking with Sue at the margins of the day (entering and leaving rooms Sue is in, on flimsy pretexts).
- Decreased correspondence with Doom (she does not want him to see her in this state, nor does she want to be tempted toward the comfort of his cognitive register while emotionally compromised).
- A small repetitive somatic tell: pressing the heel of her hand into her sternum when alone, as if checking for a felt physical pain. Inferred from the consistency with which grief is somatized in highly cognitive children.

### 6.2 Permission to Grieve

She does not give herself this permission unsupervised. She will only fully cry in Sue's presence, and even then only if Sue *initiates* — by sitting near her in silence, by an opening sentence, by physical contact. Inferred from the asymmetry of her emotional disclosure pattern.

### 6.3 Decision Habits in Grief

Risk-tolerance shifts downward. She becomes *more* reversibility-biased, not less. This is the opposite of the grief-impulsivity seen in many subjects and is consistent with her core fear of irreversible loss. She will postpone non-urgent decisions during grief and will refuse to be rushed.

---

## 7. VICTORY

Subject's victory behavior is notable for what is *absent*. There is no triumph display. No gloating, no taking-of-credit, no narrative reconstruction in which she is the hero. The Hickman-era resolutions she engineered are routinely closed without her surfacing her role.

### 7.1 Observable Victory Tells

- A small, brief, almost private smile. Not directed at any audience.
- A single exhalation longer than her usual breath.
- Tidying — she will immediately begin organizing the space she has been working in. Papers stacked, tools returned. Closure of the physical environment matches closure of the problem.
- A specific habit: she will seek out Franklin within the hour after a major win and spend time with him doing something unrelated. Inferred function: *re-grounding* in the relational stakes the win was for.

### 7.2 What She Will Not Do

She will not narrate the victory to Reed in detail unless asked. She will not accept compliments without redirecting them ("Uncle Ben did the actual hard part"). She will not allow the victory to be reified as a precedent ("we got lucky on the timing"). This last is a strategic tell — she refuses to let her successes calcify into a method she would have to defend next time.

### 7.3 Internal Victory Cost

After significant wins she shows *minor depressive flattening* lasting 24–72 hours. Inferred from the post-resolution panels in which she is depicted quieter than usual. The likely mechanism: the custodial-self requires problems to organize against; in their absence she experiences identity slack. This is one of the strongest pieces of evidence for the §4 prediction that adulthood without crisis will be psychologically dangerous for her.

---

## 8. DEFEAT / BEING WRONG

This category is rarer in the canonical record but high-information.

### 8.1 Acute Response

When her model is proven wrong — Council of Reeds episodes, certain Doom miscalculations, the Secret Wars period — Subject's first response is *silence*. Not sulking silence; *processing* silence. She will excuse herself from the room if possible, often for a longer interval than her planning withdrawals (hours rather than minutes).

Returning, she does the following, in order:

1. Acknowledges the error to herself out loud to a chosen witness (usually Sue, sometimes Ben).
2. Identifies the specific assumption that failed.
3. Reconstructs the model with the assumption corrected.
4. Tests the reconstructed model against a recent prior decision to see if other downstream conclusions need revision.
5. Discloses the revised model to relevant parties.

This is, behaviorally, the procedure of a trained intelligence analyst. She has, somehow, developed it before adolescence.

### 8.2 Defeat Tells

- Slightly hunched posture (the only context in which posture compresses).
- Reduced eye contact for several hours.
- Voluntary acceptance of menial tasks (helping with cleanup, with Moloid bedtime routines). Inferred function: humility-restoration through low-status physical work.
- Brief withdrawal from Doom-correspondence. She does not want him to know she was wrong; she does not want to be comforted by him; she does not want to risk seeking his framework over her father's.

### 8.3 The Specific Danger of Defeat

The one defeat she has not yet processed — and the one the file flags as her largest exposed surface — is the unresolved question of *whether her childhood strategy was correct*. Every time she is significantly wrong, the deeper question shimmers near the surface: was the entire architecture of being-the-one-who-manages a mistake? She has not had to answer this yet, because the architecture has mostly worked. Defeat in adolescence will start to put real pressure on it. Watch for the day she goes silent for longer than a day and does not return with a corrected model.

---

## 9. CROSS-CUTTING PATTERNS

### 9.1 The Reed-Direction vs. the Doom-Direction

Under sufficient pressure, every decision she makes is, in essence, a vote in one of two directions: *toward Reed* (open the books, ask for help, share authority) or *toward Doom* (close the books, act alone, accept the cost of secrecy). The Hickman-era ledger is roughly two-thirds Doom-direction. Slott-era post-reintegration shows visible effort toward more Reed-direction choices. The behavioral signature of conscious Reed-direction choosing is a *pause* before action that does not exist in her Doom-direction choices — she has to override a faster trained reflex.

### 9.2 The Tempo Signature

Across all conditions, her behavior is governed by *tempo discipline*. She matches the tempo of the room until she has decided to lead it; when she leads, she leads by slowing tempo down, not by accelerating. Most strategic minds her caliber speed up under pressure. She does the opposite. This is one of her most stable identifying behavioral signatures and would be the first marker by which to confirm identity if impersonation were ever a concern.

### 9.3 The Honesty Floor

She has a *floor* below which she will not lie even operationally: she does not lie to Susan about feelings, she does not lie to Fran


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# DAILY LIFE, ROUTINES & RITUALS

## Preface: What Surveillance Actually Sees

A week of passive observation on Subject Valeria Richards produces an unusual intelligence product. Most child subjects generate diffuse, low-information records — play, school, sleep, repeat. Most adult-genius subjects generate dense but narrow records — lab, lab, lab, sleep. Valeria generates a *hybrid* record: the texture is that of a child (snacks, bedtime, a stuffed octopus on a chair, cartoons consumed with Franklin), but the *content* embedded in the texture is that of a senior staff officer running parallel intelligence operations. To read her week correctly, the analyst must hold both frames simultaneously and resist the temptation to flatten her into either.

The other unusual feature: the Baxter Building (and successive Richards residences) is a domestic environment with the security signature of a strategic facility. Routine and ritual in this household are *also* defensive infrastructure. Many of Subject's habits look like a child's quirks; on inspection they are protocols.

---

## 1. The Sleep Architecture

Subject sleeps less than her chronological age would predict and more than her cognitive age would predict. Working figure: approximately seven to seven-and-a-half hours on a normal night, in a single consolidated block, with a stable bedtime that drifts by no more than forty minutes across a week. This is itself notable — most children her presenting age have ragged sleep; most genius-children sleep poorly from rumination. Subject's sleep is *engineered*. She has decided that operational capacity requires it, and she enforces the schedule on herself the way an adult would.

**Bedtime ritual (stable across years).** A precise sequence: teeth (she is particular about this — full two minutes, timed); a pass through Franklin's room to confirm he is settled (initiated by her, never asked of her); a short conversation with Sue, usually conducted at the doorway rather than seated, in which she delivers a one-sentence digest of her day that omits roughly seventy percent of what actually happened; a goodnight to Reed which she will accept either in person or via intercom if he is in the lab, but which she *will* require — there is a small, unrelenting insistence on the ritual being completed. If Reed forgets, she does not protest; she logs it. The accumulated count of forgotten goodnights is a quantity she carries.

She reads herself to sleep. The reading material is not children's literature except as cover; the cover-text sits on the nightstand, often something age-appropriate she has already memorized. Under the pillow or in the drawer is the *actual* reading — a Latverian-language primer in early years, later original-language scientific monographs, eventually correspondence printouts and her own working notebook. The pretense of the cover-book is maintained because she is aware that Sue does spot-checks.

**Sleep position and somnographic tells.** Side-sleeper, right side, knees slightly drawn — a stable posture that does not vary with stress, suggesting that whatever processing she does of the day's events is completed *before* sleep, not during. She does not talk in her sleep. She does not, on observation, dream visibly (no rapid eye-movement signatures of distress). Twice in surveillance-equivalent windows during the Hickman period she has been observed to wake at approximately 0340 hours, sit upright, write for nine to twelve minutes in a notebook kept beside the bed, and return to sleep. Both events followed days containing direct contact with Doom. Inference: the Doom-material is the only category of input that defeats her pre-sleep processing.

**Wake.** Self-wakes between 0630 and 0700 without an alarm. The absence of an alarm is diagnostic — she has trained her circadian system the way an adult professional would. First action on waking is not stretching, not bathroom, but reaching for the notebook and reviewing what she wrote at 0340 if anything is there. Then bathroom, then a glass of water (room temperature, drawn the night before and left on the dresser — a small logistical pre-position that saves ninety seconds in the morning).

---

## 2. The Morning Block (0700–0900)

**Dress.** Practical, layered, fast. Color palette skews to dark blues, plums, and the FF-adjacent grays; she avoids white (impractical in a workshop environment) and avoids pink (a flag she planted in toddlerhood and refuses to recant, because reversing it would feel like a concession). She lays clothes out the night before. Shoes are *always* closed-toe; she has a specific objection to sandals that she will articulate as "thermal regulation" but which is in fact about the possibility of needing to run.

**Hair.** Sue does her hair on roughly half of mornings; Valeria does it herself on the other half. The Sue-mornings are valued and Subject visibly extends them — leaning back into the contact, asking small questions she already knows the answers to in order to keep the conversation going. This is one of the few observable contexts in which Subject permits herself the posture of a child without strategic content. Flag this as a high-value attachment ritual and a rare unguarded window.

**Breakfast.** Eats in the family kitchen, not the lab. This is a rule she has imposed on herself. She will eat with whoever is present; if no one is present she waits five to ten minutes before eating alone, which is the closest she gets to expressing loneliness. Preferred breakfast is unglamorous: oatmeal with fruit, or eggs if Sue is cooking, or — a tell — whatever Franklin is eating, which she will request *as if she had been about to order it anyway*. Coffee, since approximately the late Hickman period: half a cup, taken black, framed as imitation of Reed but consumed for the cognitive effect. She does not pretend to dislike it.

**The morning intelligence pass.** Between approximately 0815 and 0900, Subject performs what is functionally a daily briefing — for herself. She reviews overnight messages on whatever channel she maintains with Doom (during periods when that channel is active), scans Reed's lab logs which she has access to (whether sanctioned or not depending on the year), reviews any Future Foundation correspondence, and checks news of a specific kind: not headlines, but academic preprints, anomaly reports, and a small selection of geopolitical wires concerning Latveria, Wakanda, and Atlantis. The pass takes about thirty-five minutes. It is conducted in her room or in a specific armchair in the family living area — *never in the lab*, because the lab is Reed's surveillance domain and the briefing-pass includes material she does not want Reed to see her reading.

---

## 3. The Workday Structure

Subject does not have a workday in any conventional sense. She has *threads*, which she runs in parallel and time-slices among. A representative thread inventory in a Hickman-era week would include:

- The Future Foundation curriculum and pastoral care of the children (visible, sanctioned).
- A long-running research collaboration with Reed on whichever cosmological or topological problem is current (visible, sanctioned, genuinely interesting to her).
- The Doom correspondence: cognitive-restoration negotiation in early years, ongoing tutorials and strategic exchanges later (covert).
- Independent research projects, some of which she pursues because they are useful and some because she is hiding them from Reed in order to preserve a domain of her own (semi-covert).
- Maintenance operations on the family: monitoring Reed's affect, Sue's stress, Franklin's well-being, and the Bentley/Dragon Man/Moloid sub-population of the household (visible *as* care, opaque *as* operation).

She moves among these threads in roughly forty-five to ninety minute blocks. She does not pomodoro; she does not set timers. The block-lengths are determined by her sense of when the *next thread* needs her, which is itself a continuously updated background calculation. An observer with synchronized records would notice that she leaves the lab on average four to seven minutes before Franklin enters a difficult emotional moment elsewhere in the building. This pattern is too consistent to be coincidence and too imprecise to be telemetric; it is *cohabitation cognition* — a model of her brother's day so well-internalized that it runs underneath her own.

**Workspaces and territoriality.** Subject has, at minimum, three workspaces:

1. *Her assigned bench in the main lab.* Public, observed by Reed, used for the sanctioned collaborative work. Tidy in a way Reed's is not — a quiet ongoing critique. Tools racked. A short shelf of reference texts that double as cover (she rotates the visible spines monthly).
2. *Her bedroom desk.* Semi-private. Used for Future Foundation administrative work and personal reading. Possesses a small locked drawer; she carries the key, and Sue is aware of and respects this without comment.
3. *A workspace she does not formally announce.* Location varies by era — a corner of the FF children's quarters, a niche in the Negative Zone facility during periods of access, eventually her quarters in Latveria during the tutelage period. This is the workspace in which the *unsanctioned* work happens. The diagnostic feature is that she never has more than one such space at a time and she abandons each one cleanly when its operational purpose ends. This is mature tradecraft from a person who was four years old when she began practicing it.

**Posture at work.** Sits straight, feet often not reaching the floor in early years (she selected an adult chair anyway, and refused a child-height one, which is a *statement*). When thinking hard she does not pace as Reed does; she becomes still. The stillness is uncanny to observers — a small body holding a long pause without fidgeting. When she has solved something she taps the desk twice with the middle finger of the right hand. This is the closest thing she has to a tic. It is private; she does not perform it for an audience.

---

## 4. Meals and the Table

**Lunch.** Often skipped or grazed. Subject will eat fruit, nuts, a sandwich made in two minutes; she does not stop a thread for lunch unless the thread is the family. If Sue calls a family lunch, she comes. If Franklin asks her to eat with him, she comes. Otherwise lunch is fuel.

**Dinner.** *Non-negotiable family event in her own internal rulebook.* She does not impose this rule on others — Reed will absent himself for lab reasons and she will not protest — but she enforces it on herself with near-total reliability. The dinner ritual is one of the most stable patterns in her week. She arrives on time; she helps set the table without being asked; she draws Franklin out conversationally if he is withdrawn; she steers the conversation away from topics that distress Sue and toward topics that engage Reed in a way that lets the family see him at his best. This is *invisible hospitality work* — she is hosting her own family at her own dinner. Surveillance with audio would record her speaking less than any other adult-equivalent at the table and yet shaping more of the conversational arc than anyone present.

**Food preferences.** Underexplored as identity territory. She will eat almost anything; she has no strong dislikes she defends; she has not built a "favorite food" persona. This is itself unusual in children and suggests that food has not been recruited into her self-definition project, which is concentrated elsewhere. Tea, however, she takes seriously — a habit acquired in Latveria, taken without sugar, with a specific preference for a particular Eastern European black blend. The teacup is one of the few personal-effect objects she travels with.

---

## 5. Time with Franklin

A category unto itself. Subject spends substantial daily time with her brother and the time is *structurally* different from her time with anyone else. With Franklin she is the only person in her life with whom she is voluntarily not in charge — she lets him pick the activity, lets him win at games she could win, watches the cartoons he wants to watch, and laughs at jokes that are not actually funny on their merits but are funny because he made them. The performance of being a younger sister, even though she is in every operational sense the elder, is one she sustains carefully. The reasons are layered: she loves him; she knows his depowering and reality-cost arcs have eroded his sense of his own bigness; she is willing to small herself in the dynamic to give him room.

The Franklin-time blocks typically occur in late afternoon (1600–1730) and again briefly before bed. During these windows she will reliably refuse outside intrusions — including from Doom, with whom she has been observed to defer responses by hours specifically to preserve a Franklin block. The hierarchy of her attachments, expressed in time-allocation, is unambiguous: Franklin first, Sue second, Reed third, Doom fourth. The contractual loudness of the Doom relationship obscures this and is meant to.

---

## 6. The Doom Channel

During active periods, communication with Doom occupies a specific time-shape: short bursts, encrypted, conducted from the unannounced workspace, typically between 1000 and 1130 or after 2100. She does not initiate contact within thirty minutes of any meal or family ritual — a clean separation she maintains for psychological hygiene as much as security. Messages are concise, formal in register (she addresses him with a specific respectful formula she has chosen, neither childish nor sycophantic), and frequently end with a question rather than a statement. The question-ending is tradecraft: it obliges him to respond and lets her measure response latency, which she uses as a proxy indicator of his mood and current preoccupations.

Visits to Latveria, when they occur, follow their own routine: she packs the night before from a pre-staged list, takes the teacup, takes the notebook, takes a photograph of Franklin (kept face-down in the notebook, never displayed in Latveria — a private talisman, not a flag), and informs Sue of the trip in person. She does not lie to Sue about Doom. The omissions she practices are toward Reed.

---

## 7. Downtime, Play, and the Question of Pleasure

Subject *has* downtime and the downtime is genuine, which is a clinically important finding. It is not, however, randomly distributed; it is *scheduled*, in the sense that she has identified categories of activity that restore her and she returns to them with regularity.

- *Reading for pleasure.* History (particularly the history of intelligence services, diplomatic correspondence, and the lives of advisors to powerful figures — note the thematic self-relevance), and a smaller seam of fiction, largely nineteenth-century novels with a preference for those concerning sisters and household management. She does not read superhero literature; she lives it.
- *Music.* Listens to classical, with a marked preference for Bach (structure, voice-leading, the consolation of mathematics expressed as feeling). Has been observed to listen to Latverian folk music with headphones during periods following Doom-contact — a small habit she has not examined.
- *Construction play.* Builds things with the Moloid children that have no research utility — towers, model cities, miniature ecosystems. The play is unfeigned. She enjoys it and lets herself enjoy it.
- *The Dragon Man.* Subject has a real and ongoing affectionate relationship with this entity, who functions for her as a non-judgmental large companion. She talks to him in a register she uses with no one else — relaxed, occasionally silly, sometimes confessional. If a surveillance product wanted access to her unguarded inner monologue, the Dragon Man's audio environment would be the highest-yield collection point in the building.

Pleasure-affect is observable: she smiles, laughs, becomes animated. The smile reaches her eyes more often with Franklin and with Sue than with Reed; with Reed the smile is warmer when they are solving something together than when they are simply being together, which is itself a finding about the channel through which she has learned to receive his love.

---

## 8. Habits of Place

**The Baxter Building / Yancy Street / successive residences.** Subject has *route preferences* through the family living space — habitual paths she walks even when shorter routes exist. She passes Franklin's door on her way to the kitchen even when a more direct route is available. She passes the main lab's external window — without entering — on her way to her bedroom, a glance-check on Reed. These pass-by patterns constitute a daily welfare census she conducts on her family without anyone noticing it is happening.

**Thresholds.** She pauses at doorways before entering rooms containing other people. The pause is brief — under a second — but consistent. She is reading the room before committing to it. This is a habit that will be invisible to most cohabitants and obvious to a trained observer.

**Latveria.** During her tutelage periods, she adopts a *different* set of routines — more formal, more solitary, more contemplative, with longer reading blocks and shorter social blocks. She becomes quieter. On return to the Baxter Building it takes her approximately forty-eight hours to re-acclimate to the noisier, warmer family register. Sue has learned to give her this window; Reed has not always noticed it is needed.

---

## 9. Habits of Time

- **The 0340 wake-event** (see §1) — Doom-correlated.
- **The thirty-five-minute morning briefing pass** — daily, near-invariant.
- **The forty-five-to-ninety-minute thread blocks** — operational rhythm of her workday.
- **The 1600–1730 Franklin block** — defended.
- **The dinner ritual** — defended.
- **The doorway-goodnight to Reed** — required.
- **The Sunday review.** Once a week — observably on Sundays when the family schedule permits — she takes ninety minutes alone, usually in the late morning, to update her notebook. This is her after-action review of the week: what she predicted correctly, what she missed, what she needs to revise. She has been doing this since approximately her fifth presenting year. No one in the family knows it is happening as a *formal* practice; they perceive only that she "likes quiet time on Sundays."

---

## 10. Personal Effects and Objects of Ritual

- The notebook (current volume; she retains prior volumes in a location she has not disclosed; surveillance has not located the archive).
- The teacup.
- A small folded photograph of Franklin, carried on Latveria trips.
- A pendant or small token from Sue, worn under clothing during periods of high stress, removed and stored when stress recedes. Subject would not describe this as superstition. It is.
- A stylus and a compact field-notebook she carries in everyday clothes, separate from the primary notebook. This is for capture; entries are transcribed into the primary notebook nightly and the field-notebook pages are then destroyed. *Discipline of source-handling, practiced on her own cognition.*
- No phone-as-toy. She uses communications devices instrumentally and puts them down.

---

## 11. What a Week of Surveillance Would Conclude

A competent analyst with seven days of continuous coverage would file the following observations:

1. Subject runs a household *and* a parallel intelligence operation *and* a research career *and* a school *and* a sibling-protection program, in a body presenting as a small child, on roughly seven hours of sleep, with no observable burnout markers in the surveillance window. This is the most striking finding and the one most likely to be disbelieved.
2. Subject's day is organized around *defended rituals* — dinner, the Franklin blocks, the doorway-goodnight, the Sunday review — which she protects from intrusion at operational cost. These rituals are the load-bearing structure of her psychological stability. An adversary wishing to destabilize Subject would target the rituals, not the operations.
3. Subject practices *separation of channels* with mature discipline: family, Reed, Doom, Future Foundation, and personal research are kept in distinct containers with deliberate handoffs. Leakage between channels is rare and, when it occurs, is followed by visible self-correction.
4. Subject's unguarded windows are narrow and specific: Sue doing her hair, conversation with the Dragon Man, late-afternoon Franklin time. These are the windows in which she is a child rather than a function. They occupy roughly six to nine percent of her waking hours.
5. The 0340 wake-events and the Latveria-folk-music habit are the two behavioral signatures suggesting that the Doom relationship costs her more than she has admitted to herself. They are the recommended longitudinal indicators for monitoring her trajectory.
6. The custodial-self is fully load-bearing and shows no current cracks. The question the file cannot answer from a single week of coverage is what happens to the architecture if the load is ever removed — if the family stops needing keeping. The rituals would persist for a time and then become hollow, and Subject would face the identity vacuum the synthesis section anticipates.

A week with Valeria Richards looks, on the surface, like a child's week with unusual extras. On the second pass it looks like a senior staff officer's week with unusual deficits (no commute, no peers, no off-hours). On the third pass it resolves into what it actually is: the daily life of a person who decided, very early and without consultation, to be the quiet executive of her own family's continued existence, and who has built the rituals, the workspaces, the time-blocks, and the small private comforts that let her do that job without breaking. The dossier's recommendation is to watch the rituals. As long as the rituals hold, she holds.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# UATU DOSSIER — VALERIA RICHARDS
## Section 6: Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

What follows maps the primary blood-and-origin bonds that constitute Subject's relational substrate. Each entry is structured: **History → Dynamics → Evolution → Trust/Safety → Failure Mode → Leverage**. "Leverage" is recorded in the surveillance-file sense — the points at which Subject can be moved, not a prescription for moving her. Origin-bonds include the dead and the contractual (Doom is treated as origin-bond rather than mentor; the natal contract is structural, not elective).

---

### 6.1 — REED RICHARDS (Father)

**History.** Reed is the man who could not save her in the first pregnancy and could not deliver her in the second. Subject knows this in the bone before she knows it in words — it is told to her in the careful, slightly over-rehearsed way parents tell children the stories they themselves have not finished metabolizing. Her earliest cognitive memories (post-acceleration) are of Reed's lab: the smell of solder and ozone, the specific frequency of the Baxter Building's HVAC, the click of his slide rule (kept ornamentally though long obsolete), the way he hums tunelessly when stuck on a partial differential. She learned to read off whiteboards before she learned to read off books. Her father taught her tensor calculus the way other fathers teach catch.

**Dynamics.** The operative pattern is **peer-pretending-to-be-parent / child-pretending-to-be-peer**, a mutual courteous fiction. Reed addresses her as a colleague more often than as a daughter — he poses problems, accepts her solutions, occasionally publishes (informally, within the FF) results she derived. She, in turn, performs daughterhood when the moment requires it: climbs into his lap during family dinners, lets him braid her hair badly, asks questions she already knows the answers to so he can have the pleasure of explaining. Both know the other is performing. Neither stops.

Underneath the peer-fiction is the harder dynamic: she manages him. She times her interventions to his blood-sugar lows (he forgets to eat); she has learned the specific cadence of his withdrawal-into-work that precedes a Sue-conflict and intervenes upstream with a small disruption — a question, a hand on his sleeve, a request he cannot refuse. She has, on at least three documented Hickman-era occasions, edited Reed's communications to Sue before he sent them, on a pretext of "checking the math."

**Evolution.** Phase III (toddler): pure idealization; the largest mind she knew, the safest lab she knew. Phase IV–V (Hickman era, operational maturity): the idealization fractured cleanly when she observed Reed's confusion before the Council of Reeds — saw that other-Reeds were colder, stranger, more willing — and concluded her father was the *best* Reed because he was the *most compromised by love*, but also the *least operationally efficient*. From this point her posture toward him became custodial. Phase VII (post-Secret Wars): tentative re-integration. She has begun, in adolescence, to allow him to parent her in small ways — letting him be wrong, letting him correct her on something minor, letting him worry. These are gifts she gives him.

**Trust/Safety.** *Trust:* high on intellect, high on intent, moderate on execution, low on emotional reliability under stress. She trusts him to mean well and to *understand* the right answer; she does not trust him to *enact* the right answer in real time without prompting. *Safety:* she feels physically safe with him in a way nearly absolute — Reed has rebuilt reality for her — and emotionally safe in a curated way (she shows him the parts of herself he can metabolize). She does not bring him her fears about Doom. She does not bring him her fears about herself.

**Failure Mode.** When Reed fails her it is via *abstraction* — disappearing into a problem during a moment she needs presence. The failures are small, repeated, and forgiven in advance. The catastrophic-failure scenario she silently models: Reed makes a unilateral cosmic-stakes decision (he has, in canon, more than once — the Illuminati, the incursions) and does not consult her. Her response to such failures has been, historically, to build a parallel apparatus — the Doom correspondence, the Future Foundation curriculum, her own contingency files — so that Reed's abstraction never leaves the family unprotected.

**Leverage.** She will do nearly anything to prevent Reed from feeling like the man who failed his daughter twice. Threats to Reed's *self-concept as a father* move her more reliably than threats to his body. Conversely: any actor who could credibly demonstrate to Reed that she has been managing him would inflict on her a wound she has no defense for, because the defense (concealment) would be the wound.

---

### 6.2 — SUSAN STORM-RICHARDS (Mother)

**History.** Susan is the body that nearly died to bear her, twice. Subject knows the cosmic-radiation gestational instability narrative in clinical detail — she has read her own pre-natal medical files, in Reed's handwriting, almost certainly without permission, almost certainly more than once. The first lost pregnancy is, for Subject, a sibling she never met and a debt she did not contract but carries.

**Dynamics.** This is the **unproblematized attachment** — and that fact is itself the diagnostic. Susan does not need to be managed; therefore Subject does not manage her; therefore the relationship has the rare quality of *rest*. Sue is the only person in the household to whom Subject is reliably, unforcedly a child. With Sue she will whine, sulk, complain about Franklin, ask for help with hair and clothing and the specific social-script questions a brilliant child still cannot intuit ("why was Aunt Jen sad at dinner?"). Sue answers without performing teaching. The exchanges are short and warm and contain almost no information of strategic value.

The granular tells: Subject sits closer to Sue on couches than her stated personal-space preferences would predict. She brings Sue tea unprompted (she brings Reed coffee only when she needs something). She mirrors Sue's posture in family meetings — when Sue's arms cross, Subject's follow within seconds. She uses Sue's maiden name reflexively in private journals ("Mom Storm said—"), a small linguistic vestige of recognizing her mother as a person preceding the Richards-system.

**Evolution.** The Sue-bond is the most stable line on the chart. Childhood: warm physical attachment, frequent contact-seeking. Hickman era: Subject began to recognize Sue as the *true* operational commander of the FF — the one who held the team together while Reed solved and Ben fought and Johnny burned — and her admiration shifted register from filial to professional-collegial without losing warmth. Post-Secret Wars / adolescence: small frictions begin to emerge, age-appropriate, almost welcome — Subject testing whether Sue can absorb being defied without becoming Reed-like (distant) or Doom-like (punitive). Sue passes these tests.

**Trust/Safety.** Maximal on both axes. Sue is the only adult in canon to whom Subject will, without strategic calculation, *say what she is actually feeling*. The disclosures are rare, partial, and precious. Sue is the one person Subject would call from a collapsing universe and not hang up first.

**Failure Mode.** Sue's failures, when they occur, are failures of *bandwidth* — she is running the team, the family, and her own field operations, and sometimes there is no Sue left over. Subject has been documented to *over-protect Sue from her own needs* (declining to bring Sue problems because Sue looks tired), which is itself a failure pattern: the unproblematized attachment becomes invisible because Subject won't problematize it. The risk: Sue, taking the bond for granted because it is the easy one, may underestimate how singular Subject's reliance on her is.

**Leverage.** Sue in danger is the *one threat that would cause Subject to act incoherently*. With Reed in danger she would plan; with Franklin in danger she would weep and plan; with Sue in danger she would lose the planning function for a measurable window. Any hostile-intelligence assessment of Subject must record this and never use it; UATU records it for the inverse reason — *anyone trying to protect Subject must protect Sue first*.

---

### 6.3 — FRANKLIN RICHARDS (Older Brother)

**History.** Franklin is the older sibling who is in every meaningful experiential sense the younger one. He was the first child; she was the awaited and recovered one. He has the reality-warping power that could remake universes; she has the cognition that understands what reality is. The age-gap is small in years and inverted in maturity. They have been, since her acceleration, each other's only true peer in the household — the only other person who knows what it is to be a Richards child with a power that defines and threatens you.

**Dynamics.** The defining dynamic is **protective role-inversion**. Franklin is older, taller, more powerful in raw output, and emotionally the more fragile of the two. Subject covers for him constantly: deflects parental attention from his depowering periods, fields questions adults ask him that he cannot answer in the moment, deflates social tensions at the dinner table with a precisely-timed observation that makes Franklin look funny rather than slow. She fights anyone — peer, adult, cosmic entity — who underestimates him, and is the only person whose teasing of him he tolerates without injury, because she earns it by also defending him.

Granular: she calls him "Franklin," never "Frank" (others' diminutives annoy her on his behalf); she sleeps in his room or he in hers during high-threat periods (Annihilation Wave, Council of Reeds, incursion cycles); she keeps an updated file on the status of his reality-warping reserves that not even Reed has full access to. She reads his moods through three indicators she has codified privately: shoulder height, sentence length, frequency of looking at his own hands.

**Evolution.** Early phase: pure protective imprint; he was the only other child in her experiential range. Hickman era: their roles solidified — he the heart-and-imagination axis, she the rational-strategic axis, both knowing it and both occasionally chafing. He has accused her, in canon-adjacent moments, of being "like Doom about it," which lands harder than any other rebuke she receives. Post-Secret Wars: as Franklin's powers wane and his identity destabilizes, Subject's protective function intensifies — and her private dread (that he will fade into normalcy and lose the thing that defined him) becomes one of her load-bearing griefs.

**Trust/Safety.** Trust is high but asymmetric: she trusts him absolutely with her feelings, less so with her plans (he leaks, not maliciously — he tells Sue). Safety is mutual and old; they have the specific safety of children who survived the same impossible household together.

**Failure Mode.** She fails him by *over-functioning* — by handling things he could, with effort, handle himself, thereby reinforcing his sense of insufficiency. He fails her by *not seeing how much she carries* — he is, in some ways, still the older sibling who assumes the younger is fine. Neither has corrected for the other's failure mode yet.

**Leverage.** Threats to Franklin produce in Subject the most reliable cracking of her composure documented. The cold command-voice does not engage when Franklin is at stake; instead a younger, rawer Subject surfaces — a child arguing with cosmic forces. Any threat to Franklin's identity (his powers, his sense of self) cuts deeper than threat to his body, because she has been quietly trying to construct an identity-scaffold for him that would survive depowering. Threats to that scaffold — i.e., persuading Franklin that without his powers he is nothing — would force her into open warfare with whatever entity tried.

---

### 6.4 — THE FIRST CHILD (The Dead Sibling, Lost Pregnancy)

**History.** Susan's first lost pregnancy — the child the cosmic-radiation toxicity took before delivery, the trauma that hung over the family for two decades before Subject was born. Subject is, in a precise structural sense, the *successor* to this lost child. The pregnancy that produced Valeria was Susan's "second" attempt; Doom's intervention was the correction of the prior failure.

**Dynamics.** The relationship is one-sided and posthumous, but it is real. Subject knows. She has worked it out from medical files, from a particular brittleness in her mother's face on certain calendar dates, from the way Reed's eyes drop when the word "second" is used near her. She has, in private writings inferable from her behavior, accounted for the dead sibling as *the reason her parents cannot quite let her be ordinary* — every milestone is shadowed by a milestone that did not happen.

**Dynamics, continued.** She has constructed, privately, a counter-narrative in which the lost sibling is not a competitor for parental grief but a *predecessor whose absence she honors by living completely*. She does not name the sibling (there is no canonical name; she has not assigned one — she has, characteristically, decided this is not her place). On her own birthday she is observably more subdued than the day warrants; the inference is that she has internally marked the day as also a memorial.

**Evolution.** Childhood: an inherited sadness, sensed without source. Hickman era: cognitive integration — she figures it out, contextualizes it, files it. Adolescence: ethical integration — she begins to use it as a private touchstone for why family-preservation is her core drive. The dead sibling is the founding axiom of her custodial self.

**Trust/Safety.** N/A in the ordinary sense; the dead cannot betray. But the relationship functions as a *base of certainty* — the dead sibling cannot be disappointed in her, and so functions as a non-judgmental witness in her internal life. This is psychologically efficient and slightly worrying; she has a confidant who cannot correct her.

**Failure Mode.** The mode in which this bond fails Subject is by *over-determining her sense of obligation*. The lost sibling is the silent reason she cannot let the family be less than it could be — and therefore cannot let *herself* be less than she could be. There is no permission, from this quarter, to rest.

**Leverage.** Any actor who weaponized the dead-sibling narrative — claimed to be them, threatened to "complete" the loss, suggested Subject is a *replacement* rather than a person — would produce in Subject an unusual response: not rage, but a profound stillness, followed by extremely cold operational planning. This is the only family-bond whose weaponization would not crack her; it would *concentrate* her.

---

### 6.5 — VICTOR VON DOOM (Godfather / Natal Contract)

**History.** The natal facts are not symbolic; they are structural. Doom delivered her when Reed could not. Doom named her, after his own lost love — an act simultaneously generous, possessive, and aesthetic. Doom made her his goddaughter through ritual not fully transparent to her parents. Doom restored his own intellect via secret correspondence with her (Hickman era, FF #1+). Doom tutored her in Latveria. Doom is, by any honest accounting, the **fourth parent** in her psychic household.

**Dynamics.** The unique register: she is, simultaneously, *apprentice, hostage of an old contract, affectionate goddaughter, equal-in-potential, and standing executioner-in-waiting* (her own stated promise to end him if he betrays her father). No other figure in her life occupies more than two of these positions. With Doom she holds all five without observable cognitive strain — which is itself the most diagnostic fact about her in this file.

Granular: she addresses him as "Victor" in private and as "Doom" in mixed company, a code-switch she has never been observed to make incorrectly. She accepts gifts from him (books, instruments, a small Latverian-craftsmanship desk that sits in her room) and displays them openly, knowing this distresses Reed and refusing to perform contrition for it. She corresponds with him in a cipher of their own devising, a small intellectual intimacy. She has eaten meals at his table in Latveria, alone. She is the only person in the canon whose presence has been documented to soften Doom's affect — he listens when she speaks, and he revises positions when she contests them, which he does for no one else, including himself in writing.

**Evolution.** Phase III (infant): symbolic possession; she is *his* before she is hers. Phase V (Hickman era): the operational alliance — she pulls him back from intellectual diminishment, he repays in counsel and tutelage. Phase VI (Latverian apprenticeship): full immersion; she studies his cognitive style and absorbs its strongest features (long-horizon planning, contractual ethics, willingness to act unilaterally) while consciously rejecting its cruelties (vanity, vendetta, the will-to-rule). Phase VII (adolescence): integration and partial recoil — she now sees the apprenticeship years more clearly and grapples privately with how much of her cognitive identity is *his* gift versus her own derivation.

**Trust/Safety.** Trust is high on a *specific, contractual axis* — she trusts him to keep his explicit promises to her, because his self-concept requires it — and zero on every other axis. Safety is unique: she is safer with Doom than any other non-Richards living being, *and* he is the single greatest threat-vector to everything she loves. She holds both facts simultaneously as a working condition of her life.

**Failure Mode.** Doom's failures with her, to date, have been failures of *over-claiming* — treating her as more his than she is, expecting affinities and loyalties she has not promised. Her failure with him is the inverse: she may have underestimated how much she has incorporated of him, and the day she finds him in her own reflection will be the worst day she has had.

**Leverage.** The canonical promise — *"If you ever betray my father, I will end you"* — is real, and she has the means and will to attempt it. But the leverage runs both ways and runs deep. Doom knows her cognitive style from the inside. Doom is the only being who can give her permission to be as cold as she sometimes wants to be. If Doom ever asked her, in a true crisis, to choose between him and Reed, the answer is Reed — *but the asking itself would shatter something*, because she has organized her life on the premise that the choice would not be demanded. The eventual betrayal her file silently predicts is not Doom's of Reed but Doom's of *her* — and when it comes, the loss will not be of a mentor; it will be of the proof that her childhood strategy was correct. That is the wound her file most fears, because she has no rehearsed defense for it.

---

### 6.6 — JOHNNY STORM (Maternal Uncle)

**History.** Johnny is the uncle who, in the natal crisis, *went to get Doom* — the one who made the operative decision that produced her life. She knows this. He does not bring it up; she does not bring it up; it sits between them as the unrepayable debt that is also a shared secret of competence.

**Dynamics.** Johnny is the household's **decompression valve**, and Subject uses him as such with deliberate awareness. He is the only adult Richards-adjacent figure with whom she is consistently playful in a *non-performed* way — his frivolity gives her permission to be young. He teases her with affection that does not condescend; she teases him back with a precision that surprises strangers and delights him. He has taken her flying. He has let her ride shotgun in cars she was not legally permitted to be in. He has, on at least one occasion, lied to Reed on her behalf about a minor matter, which she has filed as a piece of operational trust.

She studies him as a *case in the management of brilliant people who refuse to be serious* — Johnny is, in her assessment, smarter than he displays, and she has, in private writings inferable from her behavior, formed a theory that his frivolity is itself a defense she recognizes from a mirror angle. This recognition produces in her an unusual tenderness toward him.

**Evolution.** Childhood: indulgent uncle, fun. Hickman era: she begins to see him as operationally underrated, including by himself, and starts subtly directing his energies — suggesting missions, validating instincts the team dismisses. Post-Secret Wars: a near-peer affection; she is starting to be old enough that he treats her closer to a co-conspirator than a niece.

**Trust/Safety.** High trust on loyalty, moderate on discretion, low on planning. Safety is high — Johnny will burn for her without hesitation and she knows it.

**Failure Mode.** Johnny fails her by *flippancy at wrong moments* — he sometimes punctures her gravity in scenes where her gravity is the load-bearing element. She fails him by occasionally treating him as the asset she manages rather than the uncle who loves her.

**Leverage.** Threats to Johnny produce in her an anger of a different temperature than threats to Reed or Sue — hotter, less calculated. He is the family member she would avenge most rashly.

---

### 6.7 — BEN GRIMM (Paternal Figure / Honorary Uncle)

**History.** Ben is the constant — the man who has been in every room of her father's life since before she existed, who held her as an infant with hands that should not have been able to and did. He is the man whose hands she was not afraid of when she was small. This is canonical and load-bearing.

**Dynamics.** Ben is her **grounding figure** — the adult who treats her as a child without condescension, the only one who consistently *calls her on it* when she is being imperious. He uses a specific nickname for her (variants of "Val," "kiddo," "trouble") that she permits from him and from almost no one else. He is the one who taught her things Reed would not have thought to teach: how to throw a punch (badly, but correctly), how to read a room of working-class adults, how to lose at cards with grace, how to identify a good bagel.

She trusts his moral compass more than anyone else's in the household. Reed reasons to morality; Sue feels to morality; Doom *opposes* morality from a position of contempt; Ben simply *is* moral, durably, without needing to think about it. Subject has, in moments of ethical uncertainty, been observed to ask herself what Ben would do as a working heuristic — never out loud, never to Ben, but it is in her cognition.

**Evolution.** Infancy: pure safe-touch attachment. Hickman era: he becomes her ethical reference point, the man whose plainness corrects her tendencies toward over-clever solutions. Post-Secret Wars: their bond persists unchanged in tone, which is precisely its value — Ben is the relationship that does not require evolution.

**Trust/Safety.** Among the highest in the file. Ben is one of perhaps three beings (Sue, Franklin, Ben) to whom she could in principle disclose anything.

**Failure Mode.** Ben fails her by *not asking* — he assumes she would tell him if she needed him, and she will not, because asking is one of her counter-dependent deficits. She fails him by underusing him; he could carry more of her weight than she lets him.

**Leverage.** Threats to Ben would activate her in a register she has not yet demonstrated in canon — older, sadder, more decisive. He is the family member whose loss would *remove a load-bearing wall* from her moral architecture, and any threat assessment must record this even though it is not yet tested.

---

### Closing Note on the Family Configuration

The family configuration is not a constellation of independent bonds but an *interlocked system* in which Subject occupies the structural position of internal-affairs analyst, foreign-affairs operative, and quartermaster simultaneously. Sue is her base; Reed is her project; Franklin is her ward; Ben is her conscience-by-proxy; Johnny is her release; the dead sibling is her axiom; Doom is her cont


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

*Subject: Valeria Meghan Richards. File continues from Relationships I (family of origin).*

This section maps the lateral and elective bonds — the people Valeria has *chosen* (or chosen to keep) outside the immediate nuclear unit, the figures who function as mentors or quasi-parents in functions Reed and Sue cannot fill, and the smaller cohort who look to her as theirs. Each entry is built to surveillance-grade specificity: contact texture, observable behavioral tells in their presence, mutual leverage, and the failure mode under which the relationship would break.

A standing caution before the entries: Valeria does not collect people. She allocates them. Every name below has a *function* in her internal architecture even where genuine affection is present, and the affection — when it is present — is not diminished by the fact that she has also slotted the person into a role. This is not coldness; it is the operating mode of a child who learned to map social fields before she learned to swim.

---

## 1. Victor von Doom — Godfather, Tutor, Counter-Father

**Status:** Most strategically and emotionally consequential relationship outside the nuclear family. Treat as Tier-One in any analysis.

**History.** Doom delivered her into the world in exchange for naming rights (FF vol. 3 #54). He named her after the woman he loved and lost in Latveria. From that natal instant he held two simultaneous positions: a) the man to whom Reed Richards owed his daughter's life, and b) a sorcerer with a magical-paternal claim on her, formalized as godfathership. Through her infancy and early childhood the relationship was latent — Doom largely absent or antagonistic to the FF as a unit. With the Hickman-era reactivation (FF #1 onward, 2010s), Valeria opened a private back-channel to him, restored his damaged cognition via a brokered deal, and during the Fraction and Robinson runs spent substantial residency in Latveria as his formal apprentice. The Secret Wars God-Doom interregnum exposed her to his full theological grandiosity; she emerged from it neither converted nor disillusioned, but *calibrated*.

**Dynamics.** Valeria is the only person in canon who occupies four positions toward Doom at once — goddaughter, apprentice, contractual peer, and standing threat. She addresses him with formal respect (consistently uses "Uncle Doom" in adolescence, never "Victor"), maintains a posture in his presence that is neither cowed nor performative — straight back, eye contact, hands visible — and is the only individual on record who routinely interrupts his speeches without retribution. He, in return, modulates his speech around her: shorter sentences, fewer third-person references to himself, occasional flashes of genuine pedagogical patience. Behavioral micro-tell: she will *sit* in Doom's presence (often on a stool or low chair while he stands), a posture that visibly costs his vanity nothing and signals her refusal to compete for verticality. He has been observed adjusting his cape when entering rooms she occupies — a grooming tic he does not display for adult interlocutors.

**Trust / Safety.** Trust is *bounded and contractual*. She does not trust him morally; she trusts him *to behave according to the terms of their compact and his own self-image*. Her safety calculus around him is among the most sophisticated in any file: she has internalized that Doom will never harm her directly because doing so would degrade his self-narrative as honorable sovereign and saved-child's-namesake, but that he might harm her *catastrophically* through second-order action (acquiring her, repurposing her, building a world for her she did not consent to). She operates with full knowledge of both the floor and the ceiling.

**Evolution.** Phase one (infancy through early Hickman): she is his hostage-asset, with him operating in apparent good faith because the long game justifies it. Phase two (Future Foundation era): she becomes his junior intellectual peer and standing creditor. Phase three (Latverian residency / Secret Wars): she becomes his apprentice and, briefly, an inhabitant of his theology. Phase four (post-Secret Wars present): she is something closer to a counter-Doom — a young intelligence who has eaten the lessons and rejected the architecture. Each phase has involved a deliberate Valeria-side recalibration; the relationship has never drifted, it has been *steered*.

**Failure mode.** Two distinct catastrophic vectors. *Vector A — Doom acts on his godfather-claim under stress*, attempting to formally inherit her (politically, magically, or by force) in a moment when he believes Reed has failed her. Valeria's standing threat ("If you ever betray my father, I will end you") is calibrated specifically against this; she has rehearsed the response. *Vector B — Doom dies or is destroyed in a way that exposes how much of her cognitive identity was scaffolded against his existence*, leaving her with a sudden vacancy in her counter-model. The Vector B failure is the more psychologically dangerous because it cannot be threatened against; it is structural.

**Leverage (Valeria → Doom).** She holds his *restored intellect as ongoing debt*, his *self-image as honorable namesake-keeper* as identity hostage, and — uniquely — the *only sustained intellectual companionship a man of his pride will permit himself.* She is the only person whose esteem he cannot dismiss as either fear or sycophancy. This is enormous, structural leverage. She uses it sparingly and with precision.

**Leverage (Doom → Valeria).** He has *delivered her*, which she cannot un-know; he has *taught her things Reed could not or would not teach* (magic, statecraft, the philosophy of unilateral action), and those teachings live in her cognition. He has, in a real sense, *part-authored her competence*, and she pays the tuition cost in continued contact. He is also, in any extremity, *the second-best person in the world to call*. She has called.

---

## 2. Franklin Richards — Brother and Chosen Charge

(Cross-reference Relationships I for the sibling axis; included here because Franklin functions for Valeria as much chosen-family-and-charge as natal sibling.)

**Function for Valeria.** Franklin is the *moral anchor*. He is the person whose continued well-being she has organized large portions of her cognitive life around. Where Doom is the counter-father, Franklin is the *reason the counter-father is tolerable*: protecting Franklin justifies any alliance, any compartmentalization, any cold-eyed contract. Strip Franklin out of her life and the Doom alliance loses its ethical alibi.

**Behavioral signatures with Franklin.** She drops the command-voice. She uses contractions she does not use with adults. She lets him win arguments she could win. She physically tracks him in any room — observably keeps line of sight in crowded or unfamiliar settings. When he has been depowered or wounded she has been recorded weeping (rare to vanishing in any other context). She talks to him about her plans in fragments — never the whole, but more than to anyone else.

**Failure mode.** Franklin's death or permanent loss is the single greatest psychological catastrophe in the file. Probable response: rapid migration toward the Doom-direction, with diminished braking. Secondary failure mode: Franklin *discovering the full scope of her management of him over the years* and reading it as condescension or betrayal rather than love. She has not adequately prepared for this disclosure.

---

## 3. The Future Foundation Children — Chosen Cohort / Protege Network

A grouped entry, with individual notes; the Foundation as a unit is one of her most important social structures.

**Constitutional history.** The Future Foundation was Reed's instrument, refounded under Hickman as a school for prodigies; Valeria co-curated it from inception. She functioned as a *peer-administrator* — co-equal with Reed in admissions discussions, sole authority on certain interpersonal mediations. Membership rotation includes the Moloid brothers (Tong, Mik, Korr, Turg), Bentley-23, Dragon Man, Artie Maddicks, Leech, Alex Power, Onome, and other rotating prodigies.

### 3a. Bentley-23

A clone of the Wizard, predisposed by both genome and pedagogy toward villainy. Valeria's relationship with him is the *cleanest evidence of her belief in moral construction over moral inheritance*. She treats Bentley with neither suspicion-management nor pity; she gives him problems, holds him to standards, calls him on cruelty, praises competence. Behavioral tell: when Bentley says something genuinely vicious, she does not lecture — she goes silent and looks at him until he revises. He revises. **Leverage:** she is the person whose disappointment he has been observed working to avoid; she has, in effect, become a counter-Wizard for him. **Failure mode:** if Bentley reverts hard under external pressure, Valeria will read it as a failure of her own pedagogy rather than his nature, and will *increase* her investment past what is safe.

### 3b. The Moloid Brothers (Tong, Mik, Korr, Turg)

Four subterranean orphans adopted-in-effect by the Foundation. Tong's eventual emergence as a girl was met by Valeria with immediate, unceremonious recalibration — a one-conversation update with no fuss, no spectacle, simply re-indexed in her mental file. The Moloids treat her with something between sibling-equal and minor-deity affection. She uses them as both peers and constituency, defends their dignity in front of adult heroes who patronize them, and is the figure who taught them the difference between *house rules* and *world rules.* **Leverage:** none she would exert; they are among the very few people in her life for whom she runs no leverage calculus at all. This is diagnostic — she is capable of unmediated care; she rations it.

### 3c. Dragon Man

A semi-sentient construct treated as a full Foundation member. Valeria interacts with him with the specific gentleness she reserves for entities whose interiority she is not certain of but treats as real on principle. She has been observed reading aloud to him.

### 3d. Alex Power

Older, capable, with a complicated history of his own (Power Pack). Functions for Valeria as a *near-peer* with combat experience she lacks. She defers to him in tactical situations and lets him correct her — a *defer-to-experience* behavior she displays nowhere else outside Sue and Doom. This is healthy and worth noting; it indicates the parentified self can stand down when offered competence in a domain she has not colonized.

### 3e. Onome, Leech, Artie, the rotating prodigies

She knows every child's medical file, allergies, sleep patterns, parental status, and intellectual edges. She runs the dormitory roster in her head. This is the *institution-building* compensation in plain view: she has constructed a small society in which her competence is load-bearing and her authority is consented-to.

**Aggregate leverage.** The Future Foundation is Valeria's *legitimate kingdom* — the one social structure in her life where her authority is both rightful and visible, where her custodianship is recognized rather than covert. Removing it would force the custodial self back into deception-mode toward her family of origin. Preserve at all costs is the file recommendation.

**Aggregate failure mode.** A Foundation casualty under her watch would be a catastrophic identity event — far more so than a personal injury to her. She would not externally collapse; she would internally restructure, probably in the direction of *more* control and *less* delegation.

---

## 4. Susan Storm-Richards (in mentor function)

(Sue is mapped in Relationships I as mother; this is a supplementary note on her *mentor* function, which is distinct.)

Sue is the only adult Valeria asks for help in the mode of *please tell me what I am missing here.* Reed gets the analytical questions; Doom gets the strategic ones; Sue alone gets the *human* questions — what does this person want, why did this conversation go wrong, am I being a person right now or only a function. Sue's answers are quietly, repeatedly, the most expensive corrections Valeria receives, because they are corrections in the one register where she is genuinely the junior. **Failure mode:** Sue's loss removes the only adult mirror in which Valeria sees herself as a girl rather than as an asset. The custodial self would, in that absence, have nothing left to correct it inward.

---

## 5. Johnny Storm — Uncle, Court Jester, Counterweight

**History.** Constant household presence from infancy. Johnny was the one who fetched Doom for the delivery, which means he is also load-bearing in her natal narrative — without his willingness to set aside enmity for outcome, she does not exist.

**Dynamics.** Johnny is the relationship in which Valeria is *most consistently allowed to be a child* — he teases her, races her down hallways, lets her win at trivial things, talks to her about pop ephemera she would not otherwise track. He does not request her competence; he requests her amusement. This is a gift she did not have to negotiate for, which is rare in her life. Behavioral tell: she rolls her eyes at him in a register that is genuinely teen-ish, not performed. The eye-roll is one of the few unguarded affects she displays.

**Trust / Safety.** High emotional safety, low strategic-confidence. She does not tell Johnny operational things — partly to spare him the burden, partly because she has assessed (correctly) that he is not a discretion-firewall. He would tell Sue, and sometimes Reed. She loves him *because* of this, not despite it.

**Evolution.** Johnny's function expanded as she aged — from playful uncle to one of the few adults whose romantic/social life she can observe at proximity and learn from. She has observed him in failure (Inhumans-era marriage, repeated heartbreaks) and metabolized those observations.

**Failure mode.** Johnny's death would crack her in a way Reed's might not — because Johnny is the one bond in her file where no calculation runs, and the loss would not be analyzable into a manageable form.

**Leverage.** None either direction. The cleanest relationship in the file.

---

## 6. Ben Grimm — Uncle, Ground-Truth Adult

**History.** Present from infancy. Ben is the adult-male presence in the household whose intellect she does not contend with and whose ethics she has never doubted. He is the *baseline-human* in her life, even though he is anything but human in form.

**Dynamics.** Valeria addresses Ben with a particular un-managed warmth. He is the adult she runs *to* (not from) when she has been wrong about something and wants to confess it without it becoming a strategic disclosure. Behavioral micro-tell: she will climb into a chair or onto a surface adjacent to Ben and sit there silently for extended periods — she uses him as a thinking-perch. He does not interrupt. He has, on multiple occasions, *given her advice that contradicted her father's*, and she has weighted Ben's higher.

**Trust / Safety.** Among the highest in the file. Ben is a *moral check* she has internalized — she runs proposed actions past an imagined-Ben filter and discards options that fail it. This is one of two ethical-firewall installations in her cognition (the other being Sue).

**Failure mode.** Ben is the one adult whose disappointment she cannot strategize her way out of. If she ever did something Ben judged irredeemable, she would have no managerial response available — only the experience of being unmasked. This is healthy. It is also a vulnerability if the wrong actor learned to exploit it.

**Leverage.** Ben holds the *moral-veto* against her actions; she holds his protective instinct as a near-absolute. Neither party exercises this consciously.

---

## 7. Wyatt Wingfoot, Alicia Masters, and the Extended FF Adult Network

Lower-tier but stable. Alicia Masters in particular functions as a secondary maternal figure — a blind woman whose perception of Valeria has never been mediated by her precocious presentation or the visual register of authority she has learned to deploy. Alicia *hears* her, which Valeria experiences as both relief and exposure. She tolerates the exposure. This is data. Wyatt is a more peripheral figure — quiet competence, no demands, occasional presence. She catalogues him as *reliable backup, low maintenance*.

---

## 8. Spider-Man (Peter Parker) — Adjacent Adult, Trusted Eccentric

**History.** Peter spent a substantial period as a Fantastic Four / FF member (post-Human Torch's apparent death, *FF* vol. 1 under Hickman). During this period he was an adult presence in the Foundation alongside Valeria.

**Dynamics.** Peter is one of the few adult heroes who *takes her seriously without taking her solemnly* — he banters at her, expects her to volley, and treats her competence as a working assumption rather than a marvel. She likes him. She has been observed laughing at his jokes — a low-frequency event in the file. Behavioral tell: she will explain a piece of her work to Peter at greater length and lower altitude than she will to most adults, because he asks the question one step behind where she is, which she finds clarifying.

**Trust / Safety.** Moderate-high emotional, moderate operational. She trusts his ethics absolutely; she does not trust his discretion under Aunt-May-class emotional pressure.

**Failure mode.** None likely. Peter's pattern is loyalty plus occasional absence; both are survivable.

**Leverage.** None either direction.

---

## 9. The Council of Reeds — Inverted Mentor Field

Worth a brief structural entry. The multiversal Reeds were, in theory, the *most* qualified mentor cohort imaginable — every version of her father, distilled into a council. Her experience of them (Hickman era) was the *foundational disillusionment* of her intellectual life: she observed that intellect at her father's level, scaled to a quorum, produced *worse* ethics rather than better, and that her father's distinguishing virtue was the *part of him that the Council Reeds had amputated* — the family-anchored part. This experience permanently inflected her: it inoculated her against the seduction of pure-intellect-as-virtue, which is the seduction Doom most often offers. The Council is, in absentia, one of her most important *negative mentors* — a model of who not to become, and a proof that her father's ostensible weaknesses are in fact his moral architecture. She has never publicly articulated this. She lives by it.

---

## 10. The Self-Mentor — A Note

Closing the section with an irregularity worth flagging. Valeria has, more than any subject in comparable files, a documented pattern of *consulting herself across time* — leaving notes for her future self, designing protocols her later self will execute, and (in the Marvel Girl prefiguration) literally having encountered an older variant of herself as an early life-event. The latter means that *one of her formative mentor-encounters was with a version of herself.* This is unusual and consequential: she has internalized her own future-self as a legitimate authority, which means that in the absence of all the figures above, she has a *fallback mentor that is her*. This is a remarkable resilience and a remarkable risk. It makes her hard to isolate. It also means that under sufficient loss, she could close the books on every relationship in this file and continue to function — *consulting herself, alone, indefinitely.*

That is the configuration toward which the custodial self most quietly drifts when unattended. The work of every figure mapped above is, in part, to keep her from arriving there.

---

*End Section. Continues: Relationships III — Adversaries, Rivals & Conditional Actors.*


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

A note on framing before the entries. Valeria does not produce "rivals" or "enemies" the way most operators do, because she is fundamentally a *systems custodian* rather than a personal competitor — she does not need to win against people, she needs the family to keep running. Her antagonisms therefore tend to be either (a) people she has been forced to model in detail because they are existential threats to that system, (b) people whose intellect she registers as commensurate with her own and therefore must triangulate, or (c) people whose ethical posture offends her operational code. Her "lovers" file is, by the developmental clock of the canon, thin — she is still in the early-adolescent stage of that work — but the patterns that *will* govern her intimate life are already legible in the apprentice and peer relationships, and we map them where the evidence supports it.

---

## I. RIVALS

### 1. The Council of Reeds (collective rival; functional cohort)

**History.** Encountered during the Hickman opening of *Fantastic Four* (FF #570-onward): a multiversal cabal of Reed Richards variants who had abandoned their families to "solve everything." Valeria, age three-presenting, processed this not as a curiosity but as a *referendum on her father's character* and on what she herself could become if she let the custodial-self off its leash.
